HD Study Finds Money Gap At NASA
BY By WARREN E. LEARY
WC 734 words
PD 5 May 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 18
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

WASHINGTON, May 4 -- NASA has too many tasks and too little money to maintain a vigorous science program, a situation that threatens to erode the nation's leadership in space research and the goal of sending humans to Mars, the National Research Council reported on Thursday.

''There is a mismatch between what NASA has been assigned to do and the resources with which it has been provided,'' said Lennard A. Fisk, chairman of the council's Space Studies Board, which wrote the report at the request of Congress.

TD 

''We are particularly concerned that the shortfall in funding for science has fallen disproportionately on small missions and on funding for basic research and technology,'' said Mr. Fisk, a space science professor at the University of Michigan and a former NASA associate administrator for science.

NASA has come under heavy criticism from scientists since the Bush administration proposed the agency's 2007 budget, which requested $16.8 billion, including $5.3 billion for space science. But while money would be added to continue flying the space shuttle and completing the International Space Station, the science budget would be held about even for the next four years, $3.1 billion less than officials had expected from earlier estimates.

Michael D. Griffin, the NASA administrator, said he had no choice but to take money from science and from the new human exploration program to pay to keep the shuttles flying until they were retired in 2010.

The budgeting jeopardizes the administration's plan for human exploration of the Moon and Mars, the report said.

Scientists were upset not only by the loss of long-term financing but also by decisions on what to cut from the science program. NASA decided to delay or cut some missions and to reduce support for certain areas of research, like astrobiology and space station studies on the effects of microgravity.

Scientists representing different disciplines complained that they had not been consulted on what programs might be cut or reduced. Responding to this criticism, the agency asked the NASA Advisory Committee, its main outside consulting group, to convene experts from varied fields to recommend priorities.

Mr. Griffin cautioned, however, that scientists should make suggestions that stay within current budget limitations and should not ask for more money.

Mr. Fisk presented the new report at a meeting of these experts held at the University of Maryland and told them that his committee was particularly concerned that small, lower-cost space missions would be hit particularly hard and that programs to analyze and study data the agency got back from its space missions would be reduced by 15 percent.

Smaller missions and the data analysis that results in scientific papers are crucial to developing technology for larger missions and training graduate students and young scientists to guide future programs, Mr. Fisk said. These are the foundation programs of NASA, the report said.

''If you disproportionately cut there, you disproportionately cut the pipeline,'' it said.

Mr. Fisk said that because Congress had requested his group's report, the committee felt free to recommend that lawmakers allocate more money for NASA to correct expected deficiencies in science. It would take only a relatively small amount directed at the most critical areas to carry NASA science through the five years, he said.

''It would take about 1 percent of NASA's total budget per year, about $160 million, to ameliorate the damage to research and analysis, and the small missions,'' Mr. Fisk said in an interview.

A similar amount, he said, could also sustain microgravity research exploring weightlessness and its effects on a ''life support level'' until more money became available.

''We not just asking Congress for more money,'' he said. ''We're saying, You, Congress, have an agency you have asked to do a lot of things and then you turn around and say it can't have the resources it needs. It's not just NASA's problem.''

During a public comment period at the NASA meeting, several scientists and engineers asked the agency to reconsider its priorities.

William Bottke of the Southwest Research Institute in Boulder, Colo., speaking for the American Astronomical Society, pleaded for protecting data analysis from past and current missions that produces research findings.

''Sending spacecraft to other worlds without interpreting the results is tourism, not science,'' he said.

SE Editorial Desk; SECT4
HD NASA's Greedy Overseers
WC 369 words
PD 30 April 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

The space agency is struggling to mount a vigorous program of exploration and scientific research within a tight budget. So what has Congress been doing to help it along? Not much, judging from revelations that hordes of greedy senators and representatives have siphoned more than half a billion dollars from NASA's budget for the 2006 fiscal year to finance pet projects. It is a terrible diversion of money.

NASA is hardly broke. Its appropriation for the 2006 fiscal year was $16.6 billion, an increase from previous years, and Congress seems strongly supportive of an ambitious space program. But that is not enough to pay for returning the shuttle fleet to service, completing the International Space Station and developing a new space vehicle to replace the shuttles.

TD 

Congress only made things worse by requiring that NASA spend $568.5 million on 198 special interest items. That was a record for political pork and another sign of how out of control this kind of spending has become since the Republicans took over Congress, waving a flag of fiscal conservatism. A decade ago only six special items costing $74 million were forced into the NASA budget by earmarking.

Such diversions cause real harm to important NASA programs. In the 2006 fiscal year, NASA had to redirect half of its planned education spending,5 percent of its exploration systems budget and4 percent of its science budget to cover these feckless testimonials to Congressional vanity.

The science cuts were particularly galling because the projects that were stalled or shrunk had been given priority through a peer-review process, not through the arbitrary intervention of politicians on behalf of their constituents. The meddlesome legislators directed that grants rising into the millions of dollars apiece be awarded to various colleges, museums, space exhibits and technical consortiums in their home districts, in that way reducing the money available to support higher-priority research and NASA's own work force.

We can only hope that sanity returns in fashioning the appropriation for the 2007 fiscal year. Michael D. Griffin, the NASA administrator, has urged Congress to reduce its meddling this time around. Better yet, Congress should forgo earmarks completely.

HD NASA Plans Shuttle Launching in July, but Some Engineers Want More Modifications
BY By WARREN E. LEARY
WC 625 words
PD 29 April 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 9
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

WASHINGTON, April 28 -- Despite concerns from some of its engineers, NASA has decided to fly the space shuttle Discovery in July without making all potential modifications to its external fuel tank that might reduce damaging foam debris, officials said Friday.

Engineers have removed two large sections of insulating foam from the tank and are still evaluating the effects of that action, the officials said. Work will continue on redesigning or removing all or some of 34 smaller, potentially hazardous foam wedges in the future, but they will fly ''as is'' on the July flight, they said.

TD 

N. Wayne Hale Jr., director of the shuttle program, told a televised news conference that the decision to curtail further foam modifications before flying again followed a lively discussion among managers at a program meeting at NASA's Marshall Space Flight Center in Alabama. Some engineers argued that the Discovery should not fly until the small foam wedges were modified to reduce their potential for shedding.

''There was a strong, concerted opinion from several folks that we should wait until we have a good design on these pieces of foam and then change them before we go flying,'' Mr. Hale said, ''That's not without merit, and we considered it very strongly.

''But at the end of the day, it is appropriate to make one change at a time with the biggest problem we have, and then work our way to the next situation.''

Reducing falling fuel tank debris has been a priority for NASA since the loss of the shuttle Columbia on Feb. 1, 2003. Foam insulation falling from the tank during the launching damaged the shuttle's heat shield, causing the craft's destruction and the deaths of seven astronauts as the ship returned from a mission.

When the Discovery flew last July on the first mission since the Columbia accident, a greatly reduced but still hazardous amount of foam fell from its redesigned tank during launching. Afterward, engineers removed 37 pounds of foam that formed two air ramps, or deflectors, that protected pressurized fuel lines and a tray guiding cables down the tank's side, places from which the biggest pieces of foam fell during the last mission.

Mr. Hale said that removing the ramps was ''the largest aerodynamic change that we have made since the shuttle first flew'' and that its effects needed to be well understood before making more modifications.

''We're in a flight test program,'' he said. ''When you make a major change, you should fly that major change, and if you have to make additional changes, then you make them after the flight.''

Mr. Hale said a major factor determining when to launch the Discovery was completing wind tunnel tests to confirm computer predictions that it was safe to fly the fuel tank with the large foam ramps removed.

Engineers also want to reduce the shedding potential for 34 foam wedges called ice-frost ramps that keep ice from forming around brackets that connect external fuel lines and electrical connectors to the aluminum fuel tank. Ice formed around these connectors is another form of debris that can damage the shuttle.

In some recent wind tunnel tests, engineers tried a new design for smaller foam ice-frost ramps to cover the tank mounting brackets, but the design produced unacceptably large chunks of debris, agency spokesmen said. The current design fared better, they said, but more tests and analysis needed to be done.

NASA wants to fly the shuttle three times before the end of the year to get back on schedule building the International Space Station before the fleet's retirement in 2010.

SE National Desk; SECTA
HD NASA Chief Says Future Flights Will Force Cutbacks in Science
BY By WARREN E. LEARY
WC 519 words
PD 26 April 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 16
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

WASHINGTON, April 25 -- The ability to send humans into space after retiring the space shuttle is such a high priority for NASA that some space science must be sacrificed to help pay for it, the agency's administrator, Michael D. Griffin, said Tuesday.

The gap between retiring the shuttle in 2010 and flying a new manned vehicle by four years after that must be narrowed to prevent long-term damage to the space program and national security, Dr. Griffin said before the Senate Commerce Subcommittee on Science and Space.

TD 

Senator Kay Bailey Hutchison, Republican of Texas and chairwoman of the subcommittee, and Senator Bill Nelson of Florida, the ranking Democrat, repeated their concerns that the United States could sacrifice its leadership in space if it were to lose its ability to transport humans while other nations continued to do so. Ms. Hutchinson called the ability for America to continue human spaceflight ''a necessity.''

Dr. Griffin said that the new spaceship to carry humans, the Crew Exploration Vehicle, should be ready by 2014, and that the National Aeronautics and Space Administration was looking at ways to speed its development to deliver it a year or two sooner. The main limit to flying the new ship, which could take crews to the International Space Station and later to the moon, is money, he said.

NASA has reallocated money to keep the shuttle flying and finish the space station, while accelerating plans for the new ship, Dr. Griffin said. While all programs at NASA have been affected by budget restrictions, he said, a big share of the money to ready the new ship had to come from reducing the growth of science programs for a few years, in part by halting some new missions.

Many scientists have expressed outrage at the diversion of money, saying it could mean grave harm to space science.

But Dr. Griffin testified Tuesday, ''I believe that fulfilling our commitments on the International Space Station and bringing the Crew Exploration Vehicle online in a timely manner, not later than 2014 and possibly sooner, is a higher priority than these science missions.''

A long gap in American human spaceflight, as happened between the end of the Apollo moon program and the first flight of the space shuttle in 1981, could cause the nation's space industry to wither as its professionals retire or go into other fields, Dr. Griffin said. The United States' leadership, he said, could be lost if nations like Russia and China continued to send astronauts into space while the United States could not.

Therefore, Dr. Griffin said, delays in launching the new spacecraft ''are strategically more damaging to our nation's space program than delays to these other science missions.''

The Bush administration has requested $16.8 billion for NASA's 2007 budget, including $5.3 billion for space science. But the science budget would stay about even for the next four years, reducing financing for science by $3 billion so the money could go to human spaceflight.

SE Science Desk; SECTF
HD Problems Persist, but NASA Says the Discovery Can Launch as Scheduled
BY By WARREN E. LEARY
WC 1122 words
PD 25 April 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 3
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

WASHINGTON, April 24 -- A string of distracting accidents, safety incidents and tough technical questions have complicated NASA's efforts to get the space shuttle program back on track.

The shuttle Discovery is to fly again in July, in only the second mission since the Columbia disaster in February 2003. But there are continuing questions about fuel-tank foam, which doomed the Columbia and bedeviled the Discovery's return-to-flight mission last year, and about other parts on the aging shuttle fleet.

TD 

And this winter, there were so many accidents -- a robot arm damaged by a moving work platform, a dropped film canister that damaged heat tiles on the shuttle Endeavour, a small fire in the assembly building -- that last month the director of the Kennedy Space Center stopped all work for two hours and lectured employees on safety.

When work resumed the next day, a worker was killed in a fall from a warehouse roof.

Nevertheless, NASA officials insist the Discovery can lift off on time.

''There is two weeks of schedule margin to preserve the July 1 launch date,'' Administrator Michael D. Griffin said last week. ''We're in pretty good shape.''

NASA had planned to fly the Discovery in May, but decided to go for the next available launching period, July 1 to July 19, to complete modifications on the large external fuel tank to reduce the chances that it would shed insulating foam and to replace some sensors inside the tank. The delay also allowed engineers more time to deal with other lingering technical issues, like debris in fuel lines and aging electronics.

NASA needs to get at least two shuttle flights off this year to get back on schedule building the International Space Station before the shuttle fleet is retired in 2010. Officials hope to send three missions aloft this year and then fly four per year to finish the station with 16 flights, plus one mission to repair the Hubble Space Telescope, before the deadline.

In an interview taped for C-SPAN last week, Dr. Griffin said he was not worried about meeting the goal unless shuttle flights did not resume by late this year. ''If we were to back up to the end of the year, I might start to get concerned,'' he said.

The last shuttle mission was in July, when the Discovery made the first flight since the loss of Columbia and its crew of seven. The Columbia accident was caused by a chunk of insulating foam that fell from the shuttle's giant external fuel tank on liftoff and punched a hole in the left wing -- damage that went unnoticed during the Columbia's 16-day mission, though some NASA engineers warned about it.

When the Discovery flew last summer, a much smaller but still hazardous amount of foam still fell from its redesigned tank after liftoff. Subsequently, engineers removed more than 37 pounds of foam from the latest model tank, including a long, ramp-shaped section where the biggest piece of significant debris fell.

N. Wayne Hale Jr., the director of the shuttle program, said a major factor in determining when to launch the Discovery was completion of wind-tunnel tests to confirm computer predictions that the fuel tank would still be safe even with sections of insulating foam removed from external fuel lines and electrical connectors.

In the tests, engineers tried a new design for a smaller foam covering to prevent ice buildup on the tank's mounting brackets, but the new design failed and produced unacceptably large chunks of debris, the agency said. An analysis is under way to determine whether to stay with the original covering or choose a new one.

Technicians have also removed a manhole at the bottom of the fuel tank and replaced a set of sensors designed to shut off the shuttle's main engines when liquid hydrogen runs low. If a faulty sensor shuts off engines too early, the shuttle might not reach orbit. And if the tank runs out of fuel with the engines running, they would be torn apart and the shuttle could be destroyed. One of the sensors, which also had been troublesome before the previous flight, had shown erratic readings.

Other issues included whether to replace some of the shuttle's front windows because of uncertainty about whether they were properly certified to withstand high pressure. Kyle Herring, a NASA spokesman, said evidence was found that the windows had been tested properly, but three of the six were replaced for other reasons, like small dents sustained in flight.

Engineers also had to deal with the issue of ''tin whiskers'' in some computer control boxes. Guide rails that hold circuit boards in the boxes are coated with tin, and hairlike crystalline structures sprouted on some of them, perhaps because of age. Concerned about the potential for short circuits, engineers examined the parts and cleared them for the next flight, Mr. Herring said. But he added that NASA had ordered new guide rails made of a different material to be used in the future.

An additional concern for administrators at the Kennedy base is the string of safety-related incidents since the first of the year. The number of reportable safety incidents in which a person was injured or there was property damage in excess of $25,000 was 23 for the first quarter of this year, said Jessica Rye, a Kennedy Center spokeswoman, compared with 15 for the same period in 2005.

On March 17, an outside contractor doing roofing work tripped and fell from atop a warehouse at the base, later dying from his injuries.

Some NASA officials blamed part of the mishap problem on shuttle workers who were out of practice doing their jobs because of the low rate of spaceflights lately.

While preparing the shuttle Discovery for a July mission, NASA engineers have confronted an array of nagging problems.

COCKPIT WINDOWS

Scratches and concerns about certification standards led to the replacement of two of the six windows.

ROBOTIC ARM

CIRCUIT BOARDS

HD Above It All, Floating Like an Astronaut for a Few Minutes
BY By MICHELLE HIGGINS
WC 214 words
PD 16 April 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 2
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

Here's a new way to lose weight while traveling.

Starting this summer, Zero Gravity Corporation, which offers weightless flights to wannabe astronauts, plans to begin as many as seven new flights a week at the NASA Kennedy Space Center in Cape Canaveral, Fla. The flights, which simulate the feeling of being in outer space, are scheduled to begin June 24, and are the outcome of a pilot program by NASA to open its space-shuttle runway to non-NASA activities.

TD 

The outfitter, known as Zero-G, offers 90-minute weightless flights out of Fort Lauderdale International Airport for $3,750 each; www.gozerog.com [http://www.gozerog.com].

Passengers achieve weightlessness for 25- to 60-second intervals when the modified Boeing 727-200 reaches altitudes between 24,000 and 34,000 feet and, like a roller coaster, heads toward the earth and shoots up again in a move known as a parabolic flight maneuver.

A veteran astronaut accompanies passengers on each flight and explains how the Zero-G adventure resembles being in outer space. Fliers get to experience the gravity conditions -- or the lack thereof -- of being in outer space.

SE National Desk; SECTA
HD Planet Discovered Last Year, Thought to Be Larger Than Pluto, Proves Roughly the Same Size
BY By KENNETH CHANG
WC 505 words
PD 12 April 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 17
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

The 10th planet turns out to be barely larger than tiny Pluto, a new photograph by the Hubble Space Telescope shows.

The object -- still unnamed more than a year after its discovery but tagged with the temporary designation 2003 UB313 and nicknamed Xena by the discoverer -- covered an area only 1.5 pixels wide in the digital image, taken by the space telescope in December. But that was enough to extract the diameter: 1,490 miles, give or take 60 miles.

TD 

A previous estimate by a team of German researchers, based on measurements taken from the ground, had put the diameter at 1,860 miles, or 30 percent more than Pluto's 1,422 (as measured by the Hubble telescope).

More Hubble observations should pin down the orbit of a moon circling 2003 UB313, enabling calculation of the planet's mass.

The new findings, released yesterday by the Space Telescope Science Institute, which operates the Hubble craft, will be published in The Astrophysical Journal.

The new report also found that though small, 2003 UB313 is surprisingly bright, reflecting about 86 percent of the light that hits it. Pluto, itself considered bright, reflects about 60 percent of its incoming light, about twice as much as Earth.

The analysis of the Hubble image was led by Michael E. Brown, a professor of planetary astronomy at the California Institute of Technology. ''It's just crazy -- we were shocked how bright it was,'' Dr. Brown said of 2003 UB313, which he discovered in January 2005 while examining images taken in the fall of 2003.

Within the solar system, only Enceladus, a moon of Saturn, is more reflective than 2003 UB313. Astronomers recently discovered that Enceladus has geysers of water shooting from its south pole; the water, turned to ice crystals, gives the surface a sheen of freshly fallen snow.

That is unlikely to be the explanation for the brightness of 2003 UB313; it is now too far away (nine billion miles from the Sun) and too cold (minus 400 degrees Fahrenheit) for liquid water to exist.

Instead, Dr. Brown speculates, the bright surface is a result of a highly eccentric orbit that brings 2003 UB313 as close as 3.5 billion miles from the Sun. When it was nearer, 2003 UB313 was warmer and probably had an atmosphere of methane. As it moved away, the air probably froze and fell to the ground, giving 2003 UB313 a layer of ice, he said.

Astronomers have not decided whether to name 2003 UB313 an official planet or to demote Pluto. In recent years, many astronomers have said Pluto should be regarded just as a member of the Kuiper Belt, a ring of icy bodies beyond the orbit of Neptune, too small to be a full-fledged planet.

Since 2003 UB313 is almost a twin of Pluto in size, the two distant objects could eventually share the same fate of terminology.

SE National Desk; SECTA
HD Crash Is Planned in Hunt for Lunar Water
BY By WARREN E. LEARY
WC 951 words
PD 11 April 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 19
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

WASHINGTON, April 10 -- A NASA spacecraft will deliberately crash into the Moon in January 2009, in a kind of two-way bank shot that will help scientists search for water that might be lurking in deep, dark craters, the space agency announced Monday.

In part, the intent is to help find future landing sites for human exploration of the Moon, a goal announced by President Bush in 2004.

TD 

Earlier lunar missions identified abundances of hydrogen in craters near the south pole that are permanently shielded from sunlight, leading to speculation that the hydrogen was bound with oxygen in the form of water.

If areas on the Moon contain water ice, officials at the National Aeronautics and Space Administration said, they would be prime landing sites for humans.

Water can be broken apart to produce hydrogen for rocket fuel and oxygen for fuel and breathing, helping astronauts live off the land while exploring.

The spacecraft -- the first to strike the Moon since the Lunar Prospector, in 1999 -- will be part of a previously announced mission in which a larger craft, the Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter, will fly around the Moon to map its surface.

Both craft will be launched on the same rocket on October 2008, but the smaller impact craft will circle the Earth and Moon for 90 days before slamming into the Moon's south pole.

The project is called Lcross (pronounced L-cross), for Lunar Crater Observation and Sensing Satellite. Its manager, Daniel Andrews of the space agency's Ames Research Center at Moffett Field, Calif., said the upper stage of the rocket that was to send the orbiter to the Moon would be used as an impact vehicle.

When the 4,400-pound used rocket slams into a crater at about 5,600 miles per hour, Mr. Andrews said, it should send up a plume of vapor and debris, perhaps 1,000 metric tons of it, rising 30 to 40 miles above the surface.

About 15 minutes later, the trailing ''shepherding spacecraft,'' loaded with infrared cameras and spectroscopes to determine chemical composition, is to fly through the plume, taking and relaying data before itself hitting the Moon.

''We're going to see the impact,'' Mr. Andrews said, ''and then fly through the plume while looking into the crater and also looking sideways 90 degrees out into space to see the plume material against the darkness.''

Scott Horowitz, the associate NASA administrator in charge of the agency's new exploration initiative, said the opportunity for a second payload came because of a recent decision to use a larger rocket to launch the robotic reconnaissance orbiter. The extra payload capacity allowed NASA to consider supplementing the primary mission with something new, he added.

''This gave us a chance to consider additional high-risk, high-payoff science,'' Mr. Horowitz said after a news conference announcing the new mission. ''We got some terrific proposals for using the extra payload and selected one that buys us an early attempt to look at the resources that may exist on the Moon.''

In January, NASA asked its centers to develop proposals that would increase understanding of the Moon without interfering with the timing and primary mission of the orbiter, which is designed to do high-resolution lunar mapping to find potential landing sites. Officials narrowed an initial batch of 19 proposals to four before selecting Ames's polar impact mission, Mr. Horowitz said.

The expended rocket will probably be aimed at Shackleton crater, a 12-mile-diameter structure near the Moon's southern axis, Mr. Andrews said. The impact should create a crater about one-third the size of a football field and 16 feet deep.

Observatories on Earth as well as various spacecraft will also study the impact, officials said, and some amateur astronomers may be able to observe the event with their telescopes, depending upon weather and their location.

The mission will not be NASA's first effort to do geological studies by smashing robotic probes into distant bodies. In 1999, scientists decommissioned the orbiting Lunar Prospector probe by crashing it into the Moon's south pole, but it disappointed them by producing little or no debris because of its shallow impact angle.

In 2005, the Deep Impact project successfully sent a heavy copper impactor into comet Tempel 1 while a flyby craft monitored the resulting blast and ejected matter.

The robotic Moon missions are the first steps in NASA's plans to return astronauts to the Moon by 2018 as a steppingstone to going on to Mars. The Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter mission, with the new addition of the impactor, is to cost about $600 million, including the launching rocket, said Butler Hine, a NASA Ames lunar robotics official. The added cost of Lcross is capped at $80 million, Mr. Hine said.

Chart: ''Making an Impact''

NASA is planning to send a small spacecraft to the Moon in late 2008 to search for water in lunar craters. Both it and the upper stage of the rocket that launched it will crash into the Moon.

BOOSTER IMPACT

The upper stage of the booster will crash into a crater on the Moon's south pole. A 2.2-million-pound plume of material, possibly containing water, will be kicked up from the lunar surface.

PLUME / SATELLITE IMPACT

The small satellite will pass through the plume, making observations. It will then strike the Moon's surface near the booster, creating a second plume. The material from the plumes can be observed from Earth and other satellites.

Diagrams highlight the booster impact as well as the plume and satellite impact.

(Source by NASA)

SE Book Review Desk; SECT7
HD Satellite of Love
BY By THOMAS MALLON
WC 1102 words
PD 9 April 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 7
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

CHALLENGER PARK

By Stephen Harrigan.

TD 

397 pp. Alfred A. Knopf. $24.95.

ASIDE from its intermittent disasters, our manned space program would seem to offer contemporary novelists just about the most ho-hum subject matter on the whole gaudy national landscape. Given the uninspiring repetitions of the shuttle, skeptical readers must have greeted Stephen Harrigan's intention of writing a novel about ''the daily lives of astronauts'' by asking if it would be called ''The Man in the Gray Flannel Space Suit.''

Well, for one thing, the book's principal astronaut turns out to be a woman, and for another, ''Challenger Park'' turns out to be a fine, absorbing achievement, probably the best science-factual novel about the space-faring worlds of Houston and Cape Canaveral in the nearly half-century since the first astronauts were chosen.

Set between the catastrophes of Challenger and Columbia, Harrigan's story focuses on the life of Lucy Kincheloe, a conscientious, undemonstrative New Englander who, after years of waiting, has been given a spot on STS-108 (a mission that actually flew in December 2001). As the book opens, the principal drag on her career appears to be her husband, Brian, a bitter veteran of two shuttle missions, one of which carried him up for an extended stay on the rustbucket Soviet space station Mir. There, ''in a now famous diatribe, he unleashed a torrent of pent-up grievances'' to NASA ground controllers. (Students of the real-life American space program may be tempted to read Brian as a refraction of the astronaut Jerry M. Linenger.) After a second screw-up, beset with ''flagging health and fading reputation,'' Brian finds himself headed for part-time college teaching and ''Dine With an Astronaut'' bookings with tourists visiting Florida's space coast.

The longstanding emotional chill around Lucy's marriage had always been mitigated by the sense of obligation she and Brian felt to each other, to their careers and to their two small children, one of them badly asthmatic. But Brian's taut pride collapses when Lucy gets her big chance. Breaking a promise to her, he impulsively quits the space program and subjects her to a loud scene in front of their colleagues. As Lucy's training progresses, Harrigan nicely dovetails her personal anxieties with the simulation of emergencies in space. (She starts to see her daughter's fears as ''a minor mal that could safely be bypassed for the moment while she dealt with the greater danger of her son's health.'') Even so, at crucial points in the run-up to the launch she's noticeably off balance.

Lucy comes to realize that her chances of remaining with the mission will be saved or doomed by the attraction she and Walt Womack, the training team's leader, are feeling. A 50-ish widower, physically unprepossessing and at least as conscientious as Lucy, Walt poignantly regards himself ''as a kind of falconer, never leaving the earth but reaping satisfaction from the sight of his winged comrade spiraling out of sight, trained by him, charged by him, and in some way carrying his consciousness into the heavens.''

In dealing with Walt and Lucy, Harrigan isn't afraid -- many novelists would be -- to explore decency as a character trait. Struggling, in NASA lingo, to keep everything ''nominal,'' his central characters are almost as well behaved as the leads in ''Brief Encounter,'' and when desire does get the better of them, Harrigan fast-forwards past most of the sex, as if he knows it would be a waste of fuel. This is a smart decision, since his talents are better suited to depicting the relationship's emotional intricacies. And the payoff he achieves is subtle and intense; a marvel, really. The two dutiful, abashed and guilty people he's created strike me as the most refreshing literary lovers in a long, overheated time.

A veteran journalist for Texas Monthly, Harrigan has snugly Velcroed his plot to the warp and woof of actual NASA routine: the astronauts' prelaunch rituals, the minutiae of mission-patch design and the Bible study groups of Johnson Space Center employees. The density always gathers naturally, not as some data dump from a reporter's notebook. Harrigan does an especially good job with the physical peculiarities of space flight, from the weightless mosquito Lucy notices in front of her visor during liftoff to the way astronauts back on Earth will ''thoughtlessly let go'' of a glass they're holding, expecting it to ''hover in place'' instead of shattering on the floor.

The author's previous book was a historical novel, ''The Gates of the Alamo,'' and in this new one he allows the shuttle and the space station to experience their own sort of deadly siege. The plot twist that allows him to keep Lucy aloft for months instead of weeks is splendidly executed, in part because Harrigan keeps the thrills and derring-do to a sensible level. He knows that his real psychological business is to keep Lucy wan, anxious and depleted of bone mass -- while back in Houston Walt holds a vigil whose true nature he must disguise from almost everyone.

An understated style serves the author well through each planetary turn. This is a stately novel whose emotional precision is matched by the exactitude of its prose. (Harrigan's extended description of Walt's ''negligently bare'' apartment has such a bravura accuracy that it ends up seeming oddly vivid.)

What Harrigan can't fully rise above is the shuttle's own low-earth orbit. No matter how often he refers to ''voyagers'' or ''outer space'' or what it is to ''leave behind'' the earth, nothing will make that spacecraft do anything but go around in circles. Once launched, his characters remain as governed by gravity as the people waiting for them back in Texas. The mission plan for the fictional STS-108 is described, during the training of its crew, as being ''pretty close to the ideal of a routine flight.'' Its numbing, desired nominality is evident in the usual complement of inane experimental payloads the astronauts carry with them (''an automatic incubator for studying the effects of microgravity on Japanese quail eggs'').

Harrigan doesn't completely evade the issue, but he doesn't fully confront it either -- except through the fine irony that, unlike the vehicle it rides, ''Challenger Park'' actually takes you somewhere.

Photo (Photograph by Matt Lankes)

Drawing (Drawing by Lou Beach)


RF 
Thomas Mallon's books include the novel ''Aurora 7'' and ''Rockets and Rodeos,'' a collection of essays.

SE Foreign Desk; SECTA
HD Brazil's Man in Space: A Mere 'Hitchhiker,' or a Hero?
BY By LARRY ROHTER
WC 915 words
PD 8 April 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 3
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

BRASILIA, April 6 -- Since late last month, a Brazilian has been orbiting the Earth aboard the International Space Station, fulfilling a longstanding ambition here. But because of the manner in which Brazil's first astronaut was launched into space, national pride has been mixed with pointed criticisms of weaknesses and deficiencies in the country's three-decade-old space program.

The astronaut, Marcos Pontes, a 43-year-old air force colonel, has become a national idol, stealing headlines even from soccer stars like Ronaldinho. He has waved the Brazilian flag, talked with schoolchildren, reporters and President Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva, and even found himself lionized in a comic book and toys.

TD 

But many Brazilian scientists, along with newspaper columnists and editorial writers, have criticized the voyage, which ends early Sunday, as a boondoggle. They argue that the roughly $10.5 million that Brazil paid Russia to launch Colonel Pontes aboard a Soyuz rocket would have been better spent on research here on Earth or invested in rebuilding a space port destroyed in 2003 in a launching pad explosion that killed 21 scientists and technicians.

''The scientific value of this voyage is almost nil,'' Ennio Candotti, a physicist who is the president of the Brazilian Society for the Progress of Science, complained to reporters. He also described Colonel Pontes as a ''space tourist'' and a ''paying hitchhiker,'' and dismissed the astronaut's eight on-board experiments as ''simply for show.''

Government officials, however, say the gains in putting a Brazilian in space are scientific and political. They want to double the budget of the space program, chronically short of funds, to $200 million a year, and view increased public support for the effort and understanding of it as the best ways to persuade the Brazilian Congress to loosen purse strings.

''The idea is to give visibility to the program,'' Sergio Gaudenzi, the director of the Brazilian Space Agency, said in an interview here on Thursday. A decade ago, he added, ''China, Brazil and India were all at the same level, but China has surged ahead, and we have been left behind,'' largely because of a lack of sustained investment.

With more money, Mr. Gaudenzi said, Brazil would be able to build four additional launching pads at its equatorial Alcantara base, considered one of the best launching sites in the world because the Earth's rotation is faster there, making it easier and cheaper to put a vehicle into orbit. Brazil also plans to build and launch up to three geostationary communications satellites, and needs to develop its own rocket in order to do so, he said.

The origins of Colonel Pontes's voyage go back to 1997, when Brazil was invited, at NASA's behest, to be one of 16 nations participating in the International Space Station project. In return for manufacturing certain parts for the station, at an estimated cost of $120 million, Brazil would receive the right to send someone into space.

The United States was eager to have Brazil involved because Washington wants to increase scientific exchanges with a country that has excelled in several niches. Brazil is a recognized leader, for example, in the manufacture of medium-size jets, genome mapping and numerous technologies adapted for use in the tropics.

But Brazil informed the consortium in 2001 that it would not be able to meet the first deadline, in part because funds were lacking. It then missed a second deadline, and Colonel Pontes's mission was put on indefinite hold.

During a visit to Russia late last year, Mr. da Silva, rather than lose the money already invested in Colonel Pontes's training, signed an agreement that made the Brazilian astronaut a paying customer aboard a Russian spacecraft. Russia also agreed to help Brazil develop a liquid fuel rocket, the weak spot in a program that has had some success in building and launching satellites.

At the 2003 funeral of the victims of the Alcantara disaster, Mr. da Silva promised that Brazil would still launch its first rocket into space by the time his term ended, at the end of this year. But Mr. Gaudenzi said that target had been abandoned as unrealistic, with 2009 set as the most likely new date for a launching attempt.

Colonel Pontes's space voyage is officially known as the Centennial Mission, a reference to Brazil's pioneering aviator Alberto Santos Dumont. Brazilians are taught that Santos Dumont, not the Wright Brothers, was the first man to fly, before cheering throngs in Paris in 1906. Colonel Pontes has even been photographed aboard the space station wearing one of Santos Dumont's trademark white Panama hats.

''Few have been the moments in which we've been as proud of a Brazilian as we are of you,'' Mr. da Silva, who must win an October election if he is to have a second term, said during a televised 14-minute conversation with the astronaut on Wednesday. ''What we are spending is little compared to what this could represent for Brazilian space policy.''

Photos: Col. Marcos Pontes in antigravity training. Critics say the money Brazil paid to Russia for his trip to space could have been better spent. (Photo by Associated Press); President Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva, right, in a televised chat on Wednesday with Colonel Pontes, who is to return to Earth tomorrow. (Photo by Eraldo Peres/Associated Press)

CLM INNOVATION
SE Circuits; SECTG
HD Like to Tinker? NASA's Looking for You
BY By NOAH SHACHTMAN
WC 1373 words
PD 5 April 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 3
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

STEVE JONES doesn't have a workshop, exactly, for his miniature space elevator; he is designing it in his dorm room and in four labs scattered across the University of British Columbia.

He doesn't have a staff, either; a collection of friends and fellow space enthusiasts volunteer to help. And his budget, in the low five figures, comes mostly from the school activities fund, although Red Bull is donating some energy drinks.

TD 

But he might soon have a chance to join the ranks of the aerospace establishment by getting money from NASA and, in his own way, helping explore the solar system. To get ready, he is spending 60 hours a week on his elevator, which is meant to haul people and gear into orbit without a rocket. He has even put off graduation until the project is done.

Until recently, the chances that a college senior like Mr. Jones would contribute to the NASA space program were remote. Contracts belonged mostly to the Boeings of the world. Tinkerers and students were kept at the far edge of the periphery. But with budgets tightening and the obstacles to human space exploration looking more daunting, NASA is enlisting the expertise of outsiders.

For example, the agency is offering 13 contests, which it calls Centennial Challenges, that anyone can enter. The prizes range from $200,000 to more than $5 million, for building gear as diverse as solar sails, lunar excavators and the tiny elevators.

But more important than the cash prizes, contestants and administrators say, is the opportunity to sidestep the traditional ways NASA has done business and bring some fresh faces to its ranks.

''With a regular contract, a small group of students like us wouldn't have a chance,'' Mr. Jones said. ''This way, anyone with a good idea can contribute.''

Mr. Jones hadn't thought much about contests until the X Prize, the $10 million competition to get private spacecraft into suborbital flight. He was drawn to the idea that entrepreneurs could go into space. So when NASA announced its first Centennial Challenges, Mr. Jones signed up.

Competitors in the Beam Power Challenge -- which includes the elevator component -- had to make a two-foot-tall machine powered by light or microwaves that could crawl up a 200-foot rubber-coated fiber ribbon. Space enthusiasts hope that such a machine -- an elevator, of sorts -- could one day reach 62,000 miles into the sky, delivering people and packages into orbit at a fraction of the cost of today's launchings. The winner would be, in effect, one of the space elevator's earliest drafts.

When the contest was held last October, none of the eight entrants made it all the way up the ribbon. But Mr. Jones's Snowstar machine traveled farthest, all of 20 feet. The hexagonal array of solar cells, powering two pairs of rollers that shimmied up the ribbon, was judged Most Likely to Win in the 2006 challenge, set for August. The prize has been increased to $250,000 this year, from $50,000. To win, the climbers must make it up the ribbon in less than a minute. So far, 19 teams have signed up, nearly twice as many as in 2005.

Another well-known high-tech contest, the Darpa Grand Challenge, also had disappointing results when it started in 2004. The all-robot off-road rally, sponsored by the Pentagon's fringe science arm, the Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency, or Darpa, attracted dozens of competitors. But none of the vehicles made it past the seventh mile of the 150-mile course, largely because of navigation problems.

A year later, five unmanned cars crossed the finish line. That included robots from leading universities like Stanford and Carnegie Mellon as well as one from the Gray Insurance Company, which had no experience in robotics but whose owners had a personal interest in the competition. The Gray team was one of the last to enter the contest, and it had a major setback when Hurricane Katrina wrecked its New Orleans workshop.

Nevertheless, it beat out vehicles built by leading computer-science researchers and backed by defense contractors. It did so well that it has pulled some employees from the insurance side of the business, allowing them to focus on the company's new venture: robotic cars for the military and other entities.

''I never thought I'd work in defense,'' said Paul Trepagnier, a software development manager at Gray. ''I'm a Tom Clancy fan. But that's the extent. I mean, I'm just a programmer in an insurance company.''

Many of NASA's contests also center on robotics. The Telerobotic Construction Challenge, scheduled for August 2007, requires a team of machines to assemble items with minimal human supervision. The idea is to let robots, instead of astronauts, build shelters and machinery on the moon and Mars. In the Regolith Excavation Challenge, set for May 2007, an autonomous machine will have to dig through 24 square meters of simulated moon rock. A separate Regolith Oxygen competition, scheduled for 2008, will be held for robots that can extract oxygen from the stones. Some contests will be held annually; others will be one-time events.

NASA funds robotics research through conventional contracts too, and it uses Small Business Innovation Research grants to back companies outside the industry's mainstream. But the paperwork involved in the innovation research grants, called S.B.I.R.'s, can be intimidating.

''I don't have the grant-writing experience to get one of those,'' said Matthew Abrams, one of the competitors. ''The contest seemed like a better deal. And winning something like this can give us the credibility and the contacts to go after S.B.I.R.'s.''

The competitions offer economic benefits to NASA as well. The contestants, not the space agency, pay for the development. The winner of a big technology prize usually spends three times the purse value, said Carl E. Walz, a former astronaut who works in NASA's exploration systems mission directorate.

''Typically in R. & D., you pay as you go,'' Mr. Walz said, referring to NASA's outlays for research and development. ''You pay for failures and you pay for successes. Here, you don't pay until someone wins.''

NASA officials say that some of their contractors are worried that the contests could undermine their work for the space agency. NASA already has companies working on gloves for its space suits; why, then, does it need an Astronaut Glove Challenge? Exactly how good ideas from the competitions will be integrated into the space program isn't entirely clear. ''We're still writing the book on this,'' Mr. Walz said.

But within NASA, enthusiasm for the challenges seems to be growing. The agency announced six more contests in February, including $5 million for the first team that can store or produce rocket fuel in orbit and $2.5 million for the builders of a working solar sail.

If they win, both Mr. Jones and Mr. Abrams said they hoped to use their prize money to enter some of these more complex challenges, like the lunar lander competition being held by NASA and the X Prize Foundation.

Gregg E. Maryniak, executive vice president of the foundation, said he looked forward to having them enter. ''One of the biggest reasons to do this is to bring in people outside the existing ecosystem,'' he said.

''Look, a hundred years ago, a couple of pesky bike mechanics from Dayton, Ohio, bested, in effect, the government-funded player, to become the first to fly,'' he added. ''That's why you put on these things: to attract the bicycle mechanics.''

Photos: BRIGHT IDEA -- Steve Jones, a student from British Columbia, designed a space elevator, for hauling people and gear, for a NASA contest. (Photo by Bayne Stanley for The New York Times); UP AND AWAY -- NASA sponsors 13 competitions, including, clockwise from top, for designing long-duration vehicles; developing more flexible and sensitive astronauts' gloves; removing lunar dirt, which is as hard as concrete and difficult to excavate; and creating safer aircraft. (Photograph and renderings by NASA)

CLM The Basics
SE Week in Review Desk; SECT4
HD Making Up for Lost Time in Space
BY By KENNETH CHANG
WC 369 words
PD 2 April 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 2
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

When a network puts a television series on hiatus, the actors can go find work on other shows, or in the movies. But what do engineers and space scientists do when NASA puts one of their missions in mothballs?

Last week, after an appeals process, the space agency reinstated Dawn, a mission to explore two of the largest asteroids in the solar system. The decision came five months after NASA halted work on the project in order to investigate cost overruns and technical problems, and less than a month after the agency had decided to cancel it outright.

TD 

While the on-again, off-again nature of the project hasn't affected the spacecraft itself -- it sits half-built at NASA's Jet Propulsion Laboratory in Pasadena, Calif., -- it has put many of the lab's workers on a roller coaster ride.

Two-thirds of the people working on Dawn had been assigned to other projects; they will now be moved back to the mission, said D. C. Agle, a spokesman at the Jet Propulsion Laboratory.

During the hiatus, the remaining people on the project worked to provide answers to an outside team of reviewers. They also helped to resolve the major technical issues, most of them involving the spacecraft's ion engine, an unconventional design that will push it forward by shooting charged xenon atoms out the back.

The launching date, which managers had already anticipated slipping from June to August, has now slipped a full year, to summer 2007. The mission's cost has risen to $446 million from $373 million -- but those overruns were already tabulated before the project was put in limbo.

What has not changed is the arrival date at the two asteroids, which orbit the Sun between Mars and Jupiter. Because of the fortuitous alignment of the orbits, Dawn will reach its two destinations -- the asteroids Vesta and Ceres -- at the previously scheduled times: Vesta in 2011 and Ceres in 2015. KENNETH CHANG

Photo: Back on Track -- The Dawn spacecraft will be finished for a launching in 2007. (Photo by NASA/Jet Propulsion Laboratory, via Associated Press)

SE National Desk; SECTA
HD Weeks After Killing It, NASA Resurrects Mission to Visit Asteroids
BY By KENNETH CHANG and WARREN E. LEARY
WC 621 words
PD 28 March 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 13
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

NASA announced yesterday that it was resurrecting Dawn, a spacecraft that will visit two of the solar system's largest asteroids, less than a month after killing the mission.

''I'm astonished,'' said Lucy-Ann McFadden, an astronomer at the University of Maryland and a member of the mission's science team. ''I'm excited. I'm ready to go. I was still in denial that we got canceled.''

TD 

Originally scheduled to be launched this summer, Dawn will instead blast off in summer 2007. It is to go into orbit around Vesta, the third-largest asteroid, with a diameter of 330 miles, in 2011.

After several months of exploration, the spacecraft will blast out of orbit and head to Ceres, the largest of all asteroids, at 600 miles wide.

Dawn is part of the space agency's Discovery program of modestly priced missions that go from approval to flight in less than five years. Because of cost overruns and technological difficulties -- instead of traditional rocket fuels, the spacecraft will use an ion engine, firing a stream of charged xenon atoms -- NASA stopped work on the project in October.

Then, on March 2, after an independent panel found that the mission was running 20 percent over its $373 million budget, NASA killed the mission. About $257 million had already been spent, and about half of the hardware, including the scientific instruments, had been built.

An uproar followed among the project's scientists and managers, who said that problem areas like the fuel tank had been largely overcome and that the orbits of Ceres and Vista would not allow another two-for-one asteroid mission for years.

Many also saw it as further evidence that NASA was cannibalizing its science missions to pay for the space shuttle and the space station and future plans to send astronauts back to the Moon.

NASA's Jet Propulsion Laboratory, which is building the Dawn spacecraft, appealed the decision, a new process put in place as part of an agency reorganization by the agency's administrator, Michael D. Griffin. A group led by Rex D. Geveden, a NASA associate administrator, conducted a review, and in a telephone news conference yesterday, Mr. Geveden said the technical issues had now been resolved to his satisfaction.

''There's work to do,'' Mr. Geveden said, ''but the way forward seems pretty clear.''

NASA now estimates that Dawn will cost $446 million, an increase of $73 million.

Colleen Hartman, deputy associate administrator for NASA's science mission directorate, said NASA's 2007 budget already took into account a 20 percent increase in Dawn's budget, so the reinstatement would not lead to additional cuts in other missions.

Because of favorable alignment of the orbits, the one-year delay in launching will not delay the spacecraft's arrival at the asteroids.

Asteroids are remnants of the solar system's formation 4.6 billion years ago, pieces of a planet that failed to coalesce between Mars and Jupiter. Studying the largest miniplanets should provide valuable clues to the formation of the Sun and the planets.

Dr. McFadden said recent observations from Earth showed that asteroids were more interesting than once thought. ''They're not just rocks in space,'' she said. ''They're planets.''

Ceres, for example, is rounder than expected, she said, suggesting that it could have an interior structure with different layers of different materials, much like the larger planets. The roundness could be the result of an underground layer of ice that offsets the force of spinning.

Photographs of Vesta taken by the Hubble Space Telescope show craters and ancient lava flows.

SE Book Review Desk; SECT7
HD Too Much of the Right Stuff
BY By TOM FERRELL
WC 693 words
PD 19 March 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 18
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

RIDING ROCKETS

The Outrageous Tales

TD 

of a Space Shuttle Astronaut.

By Mike Mullane.

Illustrated. 368 pp. Scribner. $26.

LATE in 1986, nine months after the disaster that destroyed the shuttle Challenger and killed its crew, Mike Mullane made an appointment with Terry McGuire, a psychiatrist consulting with NASA.

Mullane, an astronaut of eight years' experience, was far from eager to consult a witch doctor. He was certain, he writes in his memoir, ''Riding Rockets,'' that he would become ''the first astronaut in the history of NASA to voluntarily see a shrink. I would be admitting failure. I would be violating the 'Better dead than look bad' commandment.''

Mullane's superiors, he told McGuire, were driving him nuts. They would not reveal how shuttle crews were chosen or even who chose them. They would not hear suggestions for improving the shuttle's safety and its performance. They would not communicate.

McGuire lent Mullane a 10-page document written by himself, called ''Leadership as Related to Astronaut Corps.'' ''In the last eight years or so, the dissatisfaction level relative to management style has risen significantly,'' McGuire wrote. The astronaut executive had become an ''autocratic power merchant.'' Bureaucratic tactics like ''inconsistency, ambiguity, silence, evasion . . . all have their place in his studied unpredictability.'' Could such things be? What happened to the Right Stuff?

In one respect, Mullane seems to be telling us, there was too much of the Right Stuff in the late 1980's. There were more astronauts -- 100 or so -- than available slots. Most of them had backgrounds like Mullane's: picked military fliers, macho men unacquainted with failure, products of a grueling selection process and endless training. Now they were miserable nothings without crew assignments, and their value for the space program was attenuated by their terror of saying anything that might jeopardize their chances.

But ah! what joy for Mullane and his like to receive at last a crew assignment, to blast off atop four million pounds of propellant, to perform perfectly for the pleasure of perfection and the preservation of life, all the while being consciously terrified. Counting down toward his second liftoff, in December 1988, he observes, ''My fear was 'off-scale high.' ''

Like everyone else, astronauts have parents, and we hear enough about Mullane's, who were supportive and courageous. His wife, Donna, was even more so. Other compulsory subjects, not so well covered when astronauting was young, include vomiting in zero G and the complications of the space toilet, a device so counterintuitive (necessarily) it took Mullane nearly 30 minutes to finish his task.

Mullane's astronaut class of 1978 included several names not yet forgotten, including those of Sally Ride and Judy Resnik. Late in the book, glancing back on a life not all that dedicated to exploring social issues, Mullane piously declares: ''By far, the greatest personal change my NASA experience had wrought was in my perception of women. I learned that they are real people with dreams and ambitions and only need the opportunity to prove themselves.'' How about that, friends of equal rights?

One woman above all attracted Mullane's attention: Resnik, 28 when he met her, a Ph.D. in electrical engineering, a classical pianist and, as he frequently notes, a looker. The Discovery mission of August 1984 was the first flight for both of them, and they had become fast friends during months of training. Readers, of course, know Resnik is going to die on the Challenger in two years, and Mullane is writing with hindsight. All the same, there's a convincing test of his moral animal in an evening alone with her at the astronauts' Cape Canaveral beach house, the only occasion when Resnik, not always a happy woman, opened her soul ''as if I were some harmless confidant.'' He did not squeeze her hand, pat her on the back or otherwise set free his raging hormones, he says. Even an ironclad feminist like Sally Ride should give him a passing grade, I think.

Photo (Photograph by NASA)


RF 
Tom Ferrell is a former staff editor at The Times.

HD Accidents Involving Shuttles at Kennedy Space Center Prompt Safety Directive
CR AP
WC 308 words
PD 18 March 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 12
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

CAPE CANAVERAL, Fla., March 17 -- Employees at the Kennedy Space Center returned to work on Friday with orders to be more careful after managers stopped work for two hours on Thursday in the wake of a series of accidents involving all three space shuttles.

Addressing nearly 15,000 employees over closed-circuit television, James W. Kennedy, the space center's director, warned on Thursday that a major accident could derail NASA's plans to complete the International Space Station and begin exploring the Moon and Mars.

TD 

''We must stop in their tracks the events that led me to call for this safety stand-down,'' Mr. Kennedy said.

In January, workers failed to lock down the space shuttle Endeavour's nose-wheel landing gear while transferring it between jacks on the floor, causing it to pitch forward. No serious damage was done.

Later, workers put too much pressure in the water-coolant system of the Atlantis, requiring repairs.

This month, the arm of the Discovery was dented by a platform being used to clean up broken glass. A few days later, an X-ray film container was dropped on the Endeavour, requiring tile repairs.

And last week, workers repairing the roof of the vehicle assembly building inadvertently started a small fire. The solid rocket boosters and the external tank, which are used to launch the shuttle, were in the building but not directly under the fire. They were unharmed, said Mike Rein, a spokesman for the National Aeronautics and Space Administration.

Until this week, workers had been under pressure to finish preparations to launch the Discovery on the next mission to the space station in May.

But NASA officials delayed the mission until at least July so workers could replace sensors on the fuel tank.

CR AP

This is the first time in recent memory that a NASA center has challenged a decision on a canceled mission, said a spokeswoman, Erica Hupp.

CLM EXHIBITION REVIEW
SE Leisure/Weekend Desk; SECTE; PT2
HD Crash, Bang, Boom Across the Universe
BY By DENNIS OVERBYE
WC 1322 words
PD 17 March 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 33
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

MY daughter, Mira, calls it the big boom, the asteroid impact that spelled the death of the dinosaurs. She's only 3 and still gets scared, or so she says, when it comes up on her favorite Disney dinosaur movie, so I don't think I will be taking her to ''Cosmic Collisions,'' a new show at the Hayden Planetarium at the American Museum of Natural History.

It's not that the dinosaur boom, as portrayed here from space, is so scary. But that boom, as the new show makes clear, is only one -- and not the most violent one at that -- of a series of cataclysms that have punctuated and continue to shape the history of the cosmos and the life in it.

TD 

Take, for example, the show's piece de resistance, in which a small planet sideswipes young Earth, blasting itself and a chunk of Earth into a spray of molten drops shimmering like blobs of mercury. The seats shake, the music screams.

That, scientists say, is how the Moon was formed. Within a month, some of the blobs had cooled and congealed into what would become that friendly pale face in the sky. And we have that whack to thank for our seasons; it knocked Earth off kilter and left its rotation axis tilted with respect to the Sun. Not to mention the tides, caused by the gentle gravitational churning of the Moon.

''Cosmic Collisions,'' narrated in a calm voice by Robert Redford, pays homage to a new perspective on cosmic history that belies the serene image of lights wheeling peacefully and predictably, stars evolving nearly imperceptibly. Sometimes things happen quickly, out of the blue, and by chance.

Two years in the making, the new show was produced in collaboration with the Denver Museum of Nature and Science; Goto Inc., makers of planetarium projectors in Tokyo; and the Shanghai Science and Technology Museum.

Accompanying the press kit for the show was a list of dozens of scientists and laboratories whose data and supercomputer simulations of everything from the evolution of stars in a cluster to Earth's magnetic field have formed the basis for the show. When we see the Sun popping off sparks and rays and arcs of incandescent gas like an angry New Year's Eve ball, we are looking at actual spacecraft images of the Sun.

When, at the end, we sail off into deep space, each of the glowing smudges that we sail past corresponds to a real galaxy mapped by astronomers and painstakingly projected on the big dome around us.

The realism is palpable. After that Moon-forming collision, which comes early in the 20-minute show, I could not endure the image of a rock intruding upon the sky without feeling an anticipatory jolt of adrenaline.

But not all the events depicted here are so literally earthshaking. Some are even microscopic -- protons colliding inside the Sun produce the thermonuclear energy that makes it shine and makes plants grow on Earth.

Others are ethereal. Waves of high-speed particles blasted from the Sun clash soundlessly in the near vacuum of the upper atmosphere with the bubblelike protective structure of Earth's magnetic field, creating electrical storms that can interfere with radio transmissions and the wavering curtains of light called auroras that decorate the polar nights.

If this show were a symphony, the section on the Sun and the aurora would be a flute solo, but most of us will have come for the crescendos, the planet-cleansing life-changing events, the big booms.

The appearance of something new in the sky, like comets, was often regarded as a bad omen, Mr. Redford says at the beginning, as we watch a dark rock appear in a dark Milky Way-lighted sky over the Adirondacks. It spouts fountains of gas liberated by the Sun's heat and then spits meteorites from its tail as it passes.

Space, we know, is big and mostly empty. The odds that an asteroid might hit Earth in any given year are only one in a million, Mr. Redford explains. Those odds might sound reassuring until we remember that Earth is 4.5 billion years old.

Tell that to the dinosaurs, whose reign on Earth was abruptly cut off 65 million years ago. It was then, astronomers and geologists have concluded, that a particularly nasty rock appeared in the sky. An asteroid seven miles in diameter, apparently nudged from its normal orbit between Jupiter and Mars, ploughed into the sea near what is now the Yucatan Peninsula of Mexico at 40,000 miles an hour.

From space we see a fireball the size of a continent. The entire Earth, we are told, baked at 500 degrees for an hour. Soot blackened the atmosphere for months, blocking light.

Most of the species on Earth went extinct, but among the survivors were our own ancestors, who might not have gotten the chance to thrive and take over without the demise of everybody else.

The question looms, as we see yet another big rock take aim at Earth (the music swells again), whether we could go the way of the dinosaurs.

Happily, that need not be the case. Given enough warning, according to new studies, we could nudge an incoming asteroid off its collision course by flying a spacecraft by it and giving it a gravitational tug. This is the opposite of the gravitational slingshot effect used to fling spacecraft to the outer solar system by sending them past planets.

And so the new rock passes without the money shot.

There is little we can do, however, about a much bigger collision fated to happen billions of years in the future, when the Milky Way galaxy, in which the Sun and its retinue reside, and the Andromeda galaxy, some two million light years away now but closing in, collide and merge.

Step-by-step computer simulations, in which each second represents 40 million years, show the two spiral galaxies, spinning like jeweled spiders, approach, flow through each other, separate as arms of stars flow gracefully akimbo and then draw together again in a double-yoked embrace, finally merging to become a giant egg-shaped agglomeration billions of years from now.

Because colliding galaxies are so vast, the stars and planets in them would probably just slide by one another, Mr. Redford tells us. What will collide, he says, is the gas and dust that fills the lanes of each galaxy. As the gas compresses and heats, those collisions will produce countless new stars and planets -- new arenas for whatever life might populate the new enlarged galaxy as it sails on into the universe's golden years.

Whoever lives there someday will be able to thank this collision for the spark of life.

Without such collisions, the Milky Way wouldn't exist. And probably, neither would we, Mr. Redford says as we cruise outward through the galaxies, a journey I was sorry to end.

Beginning tomorrow, ''Cosmic Collisions'' will be shown at the Hayden Planetarium daily every 30 minutes, from 10:30 a.m. to 4:30 p.m. (until 7 p.m. on the first Friday of every month), except at noon and 3 p.m. when the system is maintained. Tickets, which include museum, Rose Center and planetarium admission, are $22; students and 60+, $16.50; 2 to 12, $13; under 2, free; (212) 769-5200.

Photos: In a planetarium recreation, debris encircling a young Earth after it was sideswiped by a Mars-size planet 4.5 billion years ago will eventually become the Moon. (Photo by American Museum of Natural History)(pg. E33); The asteroid believed to have ended the age of the dinosaurs hits Earth in a recreation from ''Cosmic Collisions,'' at the Hayden Planetarium. (Photo by American Museum of Natural History)(pg. E36)

SE National Desk; SECTA
HD NASA Delays Shuttle Flight By Six Weeks
BY By WARREN E. LEARY
WC 365 words
PD 15 March 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 22
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

WASHINGTON, March 14 -- Additional space shuttle flights will be delayed from May until July, at the earliest, to replace faulty fuel tank sensors, a NASA official said Tuesday.

N. Wayne Hale Jr., the space shuttle program manager, said that replacing questionable sensors in the external fuel tank to be used by the shuttle Discovery would make a launching impossible during the planned target window of May 10 to May 23.

TD 

At a televised news conference at the Johnson Space Center in Houston, Mr. Hale said the next target dates would be July 1 to July 19.

''We wish it had worked out differently,'' Mr. Hale said.

However, he added, the extra six weeks will ease the hectic preparation schedule that engineers and technicians had been working under and perhaps allow for some optional work.

Even with a delayed launching, Mr. Hale said, the National Aeronautics and Space Administration could still mount three shuttle flights this year and resume construction on the International Space Station. ''I remain optimistic,'' he said.

NASA plans to fly 17 shuttle missions before retiring the fleet in 2010. That schedule will require a return to the steady launching rate that was disrupted by the loss of the Columbia and its crew on Feb. 1, 2003.

The Discovery's flight last July, the first since the Columbia disaster, was plagued by a problem with the fuel sensors. If they erroneously register that the tanks contain fuel when they are actually empty, a catastrophic engine shutdown could occur.

Mr. Hale said that although only one of four major engine cutoff sensors had given questionable readings in tests, all of them would be replaced with updated versions for safety reasons. The older sensors, made in 1996, have platinum wires that appear to come loose with movement, resulting in unpredictable readings, he said.

Other work that must be completed before the next flight includes testing recent fuel tank modifications in a wind tunnel. Foam debris caused the fatal damage to the Columbia, and NASA engineers were surprised when sizable chunks fell from the Discovery's tank last July despite corrective measures.

SE National Desk; SECTA
HD Hot Material Is Discovered In Icy Comets
BY By WARREN E. LEARY
WC 449 words
PD 14 March 2006
SN The New York Times
SC NYTF
ED Late Edition - Final
PG 25
LA English
CY Copyright 2006 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

WASHINGTON, March 13 -- Samples of a comet brought back by the Stardust spacecraft show that at least some of the icy bodies contain a surprising amount of material formed under high heat, scientists reported Monday.

Initial studies of some of the cometary particles returned by Stardust found materials believed formed near the Sun in the early history of the solar system and somehow ejected into the cold, distant regions where comets form, they said.

TD 

''It's sort of a big surprise to see such hot materials so far out in the solar system,'' Thomas Morgan, NASA program scientist for the mission, said in a televised news conference from the Johnson Space Center in Houston.

The first results from the $212 million Stardust project are being presented this week at the National Aeronautics and Space Adminstration's annual Lunar and Planetary Science Conference in nearby League City, Tex.

The 100-pound sample container from Stardust returned to Earth on Jan. 15, ending a seven-year mission. The spacecraft flew within 149 miles of the comet Wild 2 near Jupiter on Jan. 2, 2004. It extended a collector filled with a material called aerogel, a low-density silicon material composed of 99.8 percent empty space, to slow and trap particles without significantly damaging them.

Stardust also spent part of its long journey collecting the particles from stars. These instellar particles constantly flow through the solar system from far out in space; they are still in storage and have not yet been counted or examined, researchers said at the news conference.

Donald Brownlee of the University of Washington, the mission's principal investigator, said scientists had long believed that comets contained primal material from the edges of the solar system and that the material had never been heated.

But Stardust captured particles from Wild 2 that contained minerals that form at temperatures of 1,400 degrees Fahrenheit.

''The interesting thing is we are finding these high-temperature minerals in materials from the coldest place in the solar system,'' Dr. Brownlee said. Studying the chemical composition of the heated particles should determine if they are from the hot formation of the inner solar system 4.6 billion years ago or perhaps from stars farther away.

''It's a real exciting mystery story, so stay tuned,'' he said.

Michael Zolensky, the sample curator and a co-investigator at the Johnson center, said finding crystals formed by heat could support a model of solar system formation in which material coalesced in the center around the early Sun as jets of material also shot outward toward the more distant, colder regions.
